1423140591|4.0|The Percy Jackson series relies on riffing on classic mythological concepts and bringing them into the modern world (previous books have featured the entrance to Hades in Los Angeles, Mount Olympus in New York City and Hera's Garden in Oregon). In this book, Percy Jackson finds himself in the Roman demigod camp located in Berkeley after being pursued by a pair of gorgons. On arrival, his motivations are questioned and he must venture North to Alaska to challenge the threat of the titan Gaia and her giants.<br /><br />Riordan paces the book fairly well but it feels like we have tread the same ground before in his previous book, The Lost Hero, which featured an amnesiac demigod switching sides and attempting to stop Gaia. It's handled competently but the plot feels a bit recycled. This book represents the second book Riordan has spent setting up the sequel series and though they have been good so far, it still feels like a long time to get to the action.  Moreover, it suffers from the series' previous issues of relying on the source materiel to the extent that anyone familiar with Classical Mythology should be able to predict the outcome of the story.<br /><br />Still, for a young adult novel, Son of Neptune is uncompromising. The efficient military brutality of the Roman demigods is brought into the forefront and we never cut away for brutal monster deaths. By the same token, the Greco-Roman gods aren't altered to make them more \\"kid-friendly\\" and appear in their wild, cold and borderline sociopathic glory. Caught between vicious threats from the titans and the bitter indifference of their divine parents, Riordan makes it very easy to sympathize with the demigods.<br /><br />Flaws aside, Son of Neptune is very well done and a worthy addition to the Percy Jackson stable. Definitely worth a look for younger readers and even I, a twenty-one year old college student, managed to enjoy it.

B005VGWT2K|5.0|A documentary on a music celebrity can be measured by content and insight - what footage did they access and who was willing to contribute and what new light did it shed on the subject? On the first point Martin Scorsese knocks the ball out of the park. Though I didn't feel I knew George any better than before by the end, I was treated to nearly four hours of dazzling and emotionally moving entertainment.<br /><br />I watched both parts of the film at UK preview and at no point did my attention or enthusiasm flag. In fact I would have happily sat through any outtakes! This beautifully crafted film is packed with concert footage, home movies, press conferences, interviews, photos and documents that I've never seen before, even though I've been researching the Beatles quite heavily for several years for Beatles Songwriting Academy. There are interviews with (or at least footage of) everyone you would hope to see. Beatles, wives, brothers, son, Pythons and peers. Everyone from Eric Clapton to Eric Idle.<br /><br />The documentary is constructed entirely from interviews and clips without explanation or analysis. The closest we get to a voiceover is Dhani Harrison reading excerpts from his father's diary and letters to his mum. Though the film is visually stunning it's strange watching the practically square picture forced upon us by the source material. Equally quirky is the sound editing. Scorsese doesn't know the meaning of 'fade'. All the music cuts brutally, sometimes after a few seconds. Sometimes this is cool. Mostly it's odd. The film is largely chronological and there are some great juxtapositions of sound and visuals like All Things Must Pass accompanies footage of the WW2 bombers that plagued the Liverpool of Harrison's birth. The first part covers George's life up to the White Album.<br /><br />It's hard to pick out favourite parts. But Harrison's obvious delight watching archive footage of the Beatles miming This Boy, laughing and singing along, is one. The Beatles performing If I Needed Someone, Harrison playing What Is Love? with Billy Preston, and seeing the Travelling Wilburys in the studio would be others.<br /><br />There are moments of laugh out loud humour, especially TV footage of crusty professors discuss the significance of Pop music while Beatles and Mick Jagger seeth like captive wild animal in the background and Tom Petty recounting Harrison arriving at his house with a trunk full of ukeleles. But Harrison's story of how Lennon and McCartney inspired him to start composing is the best - \\"If John and Paul can write [songs] everybody must be able to\\". The Maharishi (a spiritual Joe Pasquali) and Phil Spector (a croaking, unblinking vision of craziness with a permanent twitching thumb) also provide some unintentional humour.<br /><br />Scorsese deserves praise for not going down the revisionist myth making route trodden by the Anthology series, especially as Olivia Harrison was one of his producers. Olivia is honest, though vague, about George's infidelity as is Klaus Voorman is about his drug problems. But the lack of a narrator almost makes George a mirror in which we see his world. We know he was loved, deeply, by friends - racing drivers, comedians and film makers, musicians, but we don't whether he was truly loveable. Terry Gilliam describes George as a mix of \\"grace, humour and a weird kind of angry bitterness\\" but what made him that way? Did he ever find a release from that bitterness? Was he a good father? Nearing death Harrison asked Olivia if he had been a good husband. She never tells us what her answer was.\\"What's the secret of a long marriage?\\" She asks herself. \\"Don't get divorced\\".<br /><br />It may sound strange but the highest point for me was simply hearing the music. Listening to Here Comes The Sun and While My Guitar Gently Weeps I was almost moved to tears at the transcendent beauty of those recordings.<br /><br />Perhaps the fact that the film cause me to fall in love with the music allover again is it's greatest recommendation.

B005HPA2XI|3.0|To preface, I first discovered Mastodon when I was in high school after I picked up \\"Leviathan\\" at a local record store solely on the merits of the awesome cover art. Since then I've been a huge fan and collector of their music. My expectations for all of their releases has always been high, but have always been exceeded by the bands remarkable ability to craft fantastic records with larger-than-life archetypal themes, over the top gorgeous artwork and packaging, and above all, stellar musicianship.<br /><br />Mastodon has already released 4 excellent album (5 if you count \\"Lifesblood\\"), with each album setting the bar higher for the next album. For the first time Mastodon did not top themselves. Understandably, the band's progressive masterpiece \\"Crack the Skye\\", could be considered to be nearly impossible to top and will probably remain the bands magnum opus. Mastodon knows this. So instead of trying to top Crack the Skye, Mastodon simply went in another direction with their music.<br /><br />Unlike the previously long and arduous recording process were the band placed particular focus on every little detail that went into \\"Blood Mountain\\" and \\"Crack the Skye\\" albums, \\"The Hunter\\" was quickly written and recorded. The album sees Mastodon moving in a more hard rock/ traditional heavy metal direction, although there are definitely still some proggy moments here and there. Opener \\"Black Tongue\\" and the later \\"Spectrelight\\"(feat. Scott Kelly) echo the bands sludge metal roots, both of which were pleasing to hear. Also the album is fairly lighthearted this time around; the bands signature sense of humor can easily be found in the lyrics and song titles. Otherwise, overall I'm not the biggest fan of the new direction Mastodon took...<br /><br />The bad: For one, the album feels more like a compilation of songs just thrown in together. There is no unifying theme or concept or even a good or interesting logical progression from song to song. I wouldn't go so far as to call the music on here filler material, but the songs to me do come of as very 'sameish'; it feels like the tracks were just being cranked out of a Mastodon assembly line, with no real purpose or feeling behind them. I wouldn't be a really far off assumption to guess that \\"The Hunter\\" was just recorded and released for the sake of recording and releasing a new album. I suppose to further reiterate what other critics reservations are about this album, by and large it seems like Mastodon decided to stay closer to their comfort zone; they didn't push the envelope and they did't try to touch their audience this time around, and therefore did not release anything nearly as intriguing as they have in the past. In other words, it is unlikely that I would choose to listen to this record over and over again like I would any of their other releases.<br /><br />Also, as a side note, the Deluxe Edition with the DVD wasn't really worth it. I can't imagine anyone wanting to watch it more than once, same goes for the 'augmented reality' experience. Having to download the bonus tracks with warner bros. downloading program was annoying as well... I think the jewel case version has cooler artwork anyway.<br /><br />The Hunter is not a bad album, it's just not very captivating work. The songs are well written, but are not challenging to the listener. There are some interesting moments to found but also some very stale moments. This is the first time I have had a very mixed/split down the center experience with a new Mastodon record, but I still consider them to be my favorite metal band and will support them until their next release.<br /><br />Hope this review helped.material, but the songs to me do come of as very 'sameish'; it feels like the tracks were just being cranked out of a Mastodon assembly line, with no real purpose or feeling behind them. 0553801473|2.0|With this latest book Martin has found a steady predictable pattern. It goes like this:<br /><br />Stalling, stalling, stalling , stalling, interesting, boring, stalling, stalling, exciting (a few paragraphs, not a chapter), boring, boring, boring, stalling, interesting, boring, stalling, stalling, exciting (again only a few paragraphs), boring, boring, boring, stalling, stalling, stalling, depressing, depressing, depressing.<br /><br />You come to realize that when something exciting happens, you will turn the page, the chapter will end abruptly and you will be whisked away to the most boring threads he has, even if he has to make a few new irrelevant threads to do it.<br /><br />What I have come to realize is that my initial expectations were wrong. I expected an epic adventure, but Martins intent does not seem to be to write a \\"story\\" in the usual sense. That is to say, it isn't meant to have a beginning a middle and an end. There is no character who comes from humble beginnings, works through his trials and saves Westeros in the end. What Martin is writing is more like one of those generational dramas. The characters mill about and then die and their sons and cousins mill about and then die. The value in this type of book is that it gives you a thorough look at life in an interesting time and place, but they are seldom what I would call entertaining.<br /><br />If I had known this would be a generational drama instead of an epic adventure I would not have bothered in the first place. Martin can be a great writer when he wants to, but he is in desperate need of a strong editor who can help him with plotting and pacing. I will not be buying the next books when they come out. Instead, if by some miracle he lives long enough to finish them all, I will have a look at the final chapters and decide then if there is enough payoff to justify sludging through the final books.

Well you might be saying to yourselves at this point that its worth it for the superior sound quality of digital media but unfortunately you don't even get that. Also certain sounds have completely been dropped. I mean I understand that when you have three guitars playing at once plus a bass guitar you have to lower the volume on some of them in the mix to not have it sound jumbled but you should still be able to hear all the instruments clearly. The original mix was just right as far as I'm concerned and in any case no mix should be so bad that it makes you think that your sound system is broken. Anyway if you still have a VCR player or DVD VCR combo as I do I would recommend tryingto find the original VHS tape instead, especially if you have never seen this one before. You should see it for the first time the way it was meant to be seen.

I know reviewers have wondered whether Pratchett is indeed writing his novels by himself or if there's a shadow author somewhere. I am going to give him the benefit of doubt. I believe Pratchett wrote those books, i can see glimmers of the Sam Vimes who stood up against the dragon king, and made sure his baby boy did not miss story time even when he was trapped in an underground cave, but he seems a little lost. 0205313426|5.0|I. Elementary Rules of Usage<br />1) We still need to be using the apostrophe s introjection without the consideration of the last consonant. This means the Charles' cat becomes Charles's cat.<br />2) When creating almost a list of things that contain more than just two items use a comma. For example: The boy's bike was green, white, and gold.<br />3) Use commas in phrases that would usually be parenthetic. This means if something was to be placed in parenthesis then phrase such as a direct title in the midst of a sentence as follows. If, Sir, you decide not to eat the soup that is fine!<br />4) The next thing the book covers is the idea of placing commas where two part sentences begin being introduced. For example if the words or, as, nor, for, or while, even but appear then you should preface them with a comma.<br />5) Independent clauses should never be separated by a comma, but should always be separated by a semi-colon. This allows for you to not have to use a period, but still separates the ideas.<br />6) Do not use periods in place of commas. This means do not separate sentences that should flow together.<br />7) When creating a list preface it with a colon. The example here would be: His bag consisted of three items: a wallet, a razor, and a toothbrush.<br />8) The dash is used as something stronger than comma, less formal than a colon, and more relaxed than parenthesis. This is something I still yet to feel comfortable using in my writing mainly because it is used when you feel all other punctuation is inadequate.<br />9) You want to use a singular verb after words such as each, either, everyone, everybody, neither, nobody, someone. Verbs are to match the number of subjects.<br />10) Use the proper case of pronoun. Do not use the wrong form of pronouns in your writing. For example: Howard and I stayed home.<br />11) A participial phrase at the beginning of the sentence is used to refer to the subject later in the sentence. This means that the participial phrase cannot be used to describe a subject before.<br />II. Elementary Principles of Composition<br />12) Once you choose a design in writing be sure that you stick to it. You do not want to change your style of writing half way through your writing.<br />13) Be sure that your paragraphs in your writing are there to serve the purpose of your writing. Begin each paragraph either with a sentence that suggests the topic or with a sentence that helps the transition.<br />14) Always remember to use and active voice rather than passive. This means to write from a present form rather than a past as often as possible.<br />15) Be sure that in your writing you keep your writing positive. This means try to avoid statements such as not as often as you can.<br />16) Use language that is specific and concrete. Do not overstate your sentences. Be sure that you keep them concise if possible.<br />17) It is important in writing to omit words that are needless. This means that you do not want to ramble on in your writings.<br />18) It's not bad to have some loose sentences. Be careful of using too many loose sentences in a row.<br />19) As you write take ideas of similar form and express them as close to the same as possible. The example of this is the be attitudes in Matthew 5.<br />20) Keep related words together. The author expresses this to be sure that your writing is not given over to ambiguous statements that do not make clear sense.<br />21) When writing be sure you do not change the tense of your writing. If you start in present stay in present.<br />22) Things you wish to emphasize should be stated at the end of the sentence. These are sometimes referred to as emphatic words.<br />III. A few matters of form<br /><br />The author takes the opportunity here to address forms of writing that are commonly misused. Things like headings, margins, and References.<br />IV. Words and expressions commonly misused<br /><br />This is the area where the author addresses the common misuseof words and expressions.<br />V. An Approach to style<br />1) Be sure that your writing puts you into the background. That you are not at the forefront of your writing.<br />2) There are many rules of writing, but sometimes this means that we need to step outside the rules and find our own language and style of writing.<br />3) Be sure you have a design for your writing. Do not neglect your emotions for the sake of design sometimes it is good to step outside of the mold of your writing to fully express your feelings.<br />4) Be sure you use nouns and verbs in order to fill in the perceptions of your writing. It is good to give deep description in order to make your writing pop and to draw readers in.<br />5) It is ok to revise and rewrite your works. This means you do not have to get it right the first time. Allow for major surgery on your paper.<br />6) Do not be too wordy. As many of us write we are told to make papers a certain length therefore are forced to make things longer than they need to be.<br />7) Do not put your readers on guard. Overstatement is a common fault of most writers.<br />8) Avoid qualifiers, words like little, very, rather. These create a sense of insecurity in the writing.<br />9) Do not point the writing to yourself when writing about a different subject. Readers want the information being pointed to not just the perspective of the writer.<br />10) Do not use weird spelling or slang in your writing. This creates a disconnect from the reader.<br />11) Do not tell all. It is good at times to be sparing and not reveal too much.<br />12) Do not construct awkward adverbs. Things like the word tangledly. These are hard to read and disrupt the flow of your writing.<br />13) Be sure that the reader knows who is speaking. Do not put your phrases \\"he said, she said, or he stated\\" in the wrong places.<br />14) Do not use million dollar words if a penny word will suffice. If you use big words people will know you can use a dictionary, but then you force your reader to use a dictionary to understand your writing.<br />15) Be sure that you do not use dialect unless you understand it well. This means do not use ungrammatically correct terms in your writing unless you know how to properly use them.<br />16) Again be clear and specific. Do not be ambiguous or scattered in your writing.<br />17) Do not inject your own opinions into your writing. Too many of us tend to give our opinion on the way that things should be viewed. Do not do this in your writing.<br />18) Use figures of speech sparingly. When you use metaphors make them clear and do not compare a swordfish to an hourglass.<br />19) Do not take short cuts at the cost of clarity. Shortcuts can be self-defeating in a lot of ways.<br />20) The use of foreign languages is strongly frowned upon. It does no good, but to show the knowledge of the writer. In other words write in English.<br />21) Better to write within the standard rules rather than escaping away to natural tongue and speech. Write according to the rules not off beat language.<br /><br />My Personal Opinion of the book is that it is really helpful in clearly and concisely stating the rules of writing to the reader. He uses a very clear Roman numeral construct that makes it clear in the points that he is trying to make. I really enjoyed reading the book.<br />I plan to apply as much of the book as possible, but more than anything I would like to apply the comma rules to my writing as I grow in better understanding of them. This was a hard thing for me to grasp, but would like to continue in growth in this area of my writing. I would like to be sure my writing is clear and concise as well. I tend to use a lot of ambiguous statements.<br />I highly recommend this book to others who struggle with their writing. 0307454541|4.0|Now, I'll admit that I hoped on The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo bandwagon when I heard they were finally making an American version of the film.  It took me a good 200 pages in before I actually started to get invested in this book, with all the hype surrounding it as a fast passed, intriguing, mysterious, and all around epic read I really expected it to pick up a little faster than it did.<br /><br />This is one of the only cases where I am glad I was forced to continue reading.  Once you're past the setting of scenes and introducing of characters the story really picks up.  You start to feel for Salander and root for Blomkvist.  Everyone becomes much more personal and the story starts to take shape.  There are still several chapters where Larsson's wordiness seems to take over, and there are lots of details about the economic structure of Sweden which, while somewhat relevant to the plot, don't add much if you're reading this book for the intrigue and aren't too interested in the economic/business aspect.  If I understood or could read even a little Swedish I might try reading this book in its original language to see if that does anything to make the wordiness a little more literary, but since I don't possess those language skills I just have to rely on Keeland's translation and assume it hasn't lost too much in translation.<br /><br />Overall this book really did live up to all the hype, and after finishing this book I had to read the other two immediately afterwards. 0553381695|5.0|Already there are many reviews. I would like to address some of their criticisms...  It seems many are angered by the mass of suffering dished out to the 'good guys', in contrast to how the 'bad guys' thrive. But this is not a story with clear cases of good and evil, the hues blend. still, it would be fair enough to say that, in general terms, the Starks are more sympathetic than the lannisters, but even so, the criticism rings hollow. We are reading a series. And this is but the second book of what may end up as many as eight. No matter what the fairy tale, no 'good guys' will triumph so early into the piece. First they must plunge and plunge deep. it would be a rather dull affair if they didn't. Certainly it could not hope to comment on anything existential or historical in any meaningful way, nor would it be very entertaining. Bran has suffered the loss of his legs, and in this installment he loses Winterfell and many of his people as well. But he has gained the third eye of the Seers. At last he is becoming interesting.   The same is true of Arya. She is developing nicely, and this is only possible through the suffering she encounters. She is a little too 'bulletproof (or swordproof)' perhaps, but she has gone from being a troublesome and awkward child, into a young killer. She is motivated by good, but willing to engage in cruelty. She is becoming interesting. She is becoming a product of the medieval world in which she lives.  And her sister, Sansa too. No character in the book has caused me to groan as much. She is dull. Her chapters only fascinating because they provide a lens through which we can glimpse Joffrey and Cersei. She is intersting only by way of exposition. Yet their is hope. And this is only because of the suffering which constantly besets her. The wool is beginning to tumble from her eyes as she learns that a lady's life is cruel; war is peril, and that knights exist to kill. In fact Martin very expertly is bringing her into focus; preparing her for later on. There is a moment when she sees the Hound in a sympathetic light, the first shift in her consciousness; and then, near the end of the book, there is a moment when the Hound, fleeing battle, enters her room drunk. She is faced with the possibility of cruelty, but this is the only time that i sat up and truly took interest in her story. Was he to rape her, perhaps? As it transpires, little happens, except that Sansa thinks maybe he will kiss her. I am hoping that some kind of twisted romance may develope, once more smudging the lines of what we consider good and evil.   And what of Tyrion, the anti-hero of this particular book? his suffeing, told as back story, is fleshed out, and consequently he becomes the most fully realised of all the POV's. And come the end he is left bed-ridden, missing half a nose, and scarred. Will Shae still love him? Will his whoring and fooling around, his sharp wit, be lost with his pride? It is a quiet cliffhanger, but all the more gripping because of it; it is the suffering, told so expertly and in a neutral voice by Martin, that makes his work so compelling.   Which brings me to the second criticism, namely Daenerys. Sadly she is the flaw of the work. All the sadder since she is most likely the real main character of the book, who will no doubt end up as queen of the seven kingdoms. She is dull. At no point does she feel like a real person, like Sansa, only more so, an object of exposition. After her excellent introduction in a Game of thrones, she now finds herself in something of a no mans land. There is quite literally nothing for her to do. It would be too fast, easy, and impossible for Martin to suddenly grant her the army she is seeking, but yet she must do something, and you can almost feel the author scratching his head trying to flesh something out for her. Part of the problem is that she exists in isolation from the interwoven fabric of the power struggles of Westeros which benifits the other characters and provides them a sense of urgency. Daenerys by contrast languishes. It might have been better if she did not appear in this installment at all, allowing her presence in the first to serve as something of a prologue, so that she might turn up later in the series, ripened, ready to enter the story in earnest. Instead she goes to Quarth and the whole set-up feels rushed and bathetic. Why does everyone show so much interest in her? Ah, because of the dragons. So why then do they not simply try and steal them away from her? And what of the dragons themselves? We have barely any description of them at all. No musings, no introspection; Dany does not contemplate them or their meaning one bit. And then the section concerning the House of the Undying - sure, it's brilliantly written, but their is no deliniation. Why do they try and steal her life-force, and what is life-force anyway? In what way did Pryat Pree try and trick her? It's not adequately explained.   The series also seems to be moving into the more magical terrain, and though i personally don't really wish it to go there, it is fantasy after all, and no one by rights can begrudge Martin the direction of his own story. And it is through Malisandre that these magical elements appear most pronounced. She gives birth to a shadow which slays King Renly, and then shortly after it slays a lesser lord. It is well told, as always, but it does smack of a plot devise, since after being used twice, it is then discarded when it is most needed, for the show down with the Lannisters at the end. It sticks out, I doubt the validity of the plot, a plot which usually feels so utterly real. But the biggest weakness here is not the plot devise, for it can be overlooked, it is how that plot devise relates to Martins concept of religion in general. As great and awsome as the histrionics are, his dealings with religion are crude and scant at best. This is actually a hangover from the Game of thrones - we never learn what the old and new Godsare? How are they concieved? What are their histories? what are the beliefs? And until we know this, how can we fully appreciate the danger or power of this new One God, the Light in which Malisandre has faith? It doesnt give a context for her magic.   But to anyone who is reading negative reviews here, and feels swayed, don't be. The sweep of the story is epic. It's characters beleivable. It's tone and it's concerns adult. It speaks as much for today as it does some imaginary medieval world. And as for the prose themselves, they are neutral yet gripping, controlled and assured. Oh and the scene with Asha is hilarious. But I do think that this story is more conventional than people think - it is just told in unconventional ways.
